                <meta name="dc.publisher" content="eLife Sciences Publications Limited">

                                    <meta name="dc.title" content="Independent evolution of ancestral and novel defenses in a genus of toxic plants (Erysimum, Brassicaceae)">
                
                                    <meta name="dc.identifier" content="doi:10.7554/eLife.51712">
                
                                    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-04-07">

                                            <meta name="dc.rights" content=" 2020 Zst et al.. This article is distributed under the terms of the Creative Commons Attribution License, which permits unrestricted use and redistribution provided that the original author and source are credited.">
                    
                
                                    <meta name="dc.description" content="Plants are often attacked by insects and other herbivores. As a result, they have evolved to defend themselves by producing many different chemicals that are toxic to these pests. As producing each chemical costs energy, individual plants often only produce one type of chemical that is targeted towards their main herbivore. Related species of plants often use the same type of chemical defense so, if a particular herbivore gains the ability to cope with this chemical, it may rapidly become an important pest for the whole plant family. To escape this threat, some plants have gained the ability to produce more than one type of chemical defense. Wallflowers, for example, are a group of plants in the mustard family that produce two types of toxic chemicals: mustard oils, which are common in most plants in this family; and cardenolides, which are an innovation of the wallflowers, and which are otherwise found only in distantly related plants such as foxglove and milkweed. The combination of these two chemical defenses within the same plant may have allowed the wallflowers to escape attacks from their main herbivores and may explain why the number of wallflower species rapidly increased within the last two million years. Zst et al. have now studied the diversity of mustard oils and cardenolides present in many different species of wallflower. This analysis revealed that almost all of the tested wallflower species produced high amounts of both chemical defenses, while only one species lacked the ability to produce cardenolides. The levels of mustard oils had no relation to the levels of cardenolides in the tested species, which suggests that the regulation of these two defenses is not linked. Furthermore, Zst et al. found that closely related wallflower species produced more similar cardenolides, but less similar mustard oils, to each other. This suggests that mustard oils and cardenolides have evolved independently in wallflowers and have distinct roles in the defense against different herbivores. The evolution of insect resistance to pesticides and other toxins is an important concern for agriculture. Applying multiple toxins to crops at the same time is an important strategy to slow the evolution of resistance in the pests. The findings of Zst et al. describe a system in which plants have naturally evolved an equivalent strategy to escape their main herbivores. Understanding how plants produce multiple chemical defenses, and the costs involved, may help efforts to breed crop species that are more resistant to herbivores and require fewer applications of pesticides.">

              <span class="content-header__institution">Department of Chemical Ecology, Bielefeld University, Germany<span class="content-header__institution_separator" aria-hidden="true">;</span>
              </span>
            </li>
        </ol>
    </div>

                    <section
    class="article-section article-section--first"
   id="abstract"
  data-behaviour="ArticleSection"
  
>

  <header class="article-section__header">
    <h2 class="article-section__header_text">Abstract</h2>
  </header>

  <div class="article-section__body">
      <p class="paragraph">Phytochemical diversity is thought to result from coevolutionary cycles as specialization in herbivores imposes diversifying selection on plant chemical defenses. Plants in the speciose genus <i>Erysimum</i> (Brassicaceae) produce both ancestral glucosinolates and evolutionarily novel cardenolides as defenses. Here we test macroevolutionary hypotheses on co-expression, co-regulation, and diversification of these potentially redundant defenses across this genus. We sequenced and assembled the genome of <i>E. cheiranthoides</i> and foliar transcriptomes of 47 additional <i>Erysimum</i> species to construct a phylogeny from 9868 orthologous genes, revealing several geographic clades but also high levels of gene discordance. Concentrations, inducibility, and diversity of the two defenses varied independently among species, with no evidence for trade-offs. Closely related, geographically co-occurring species shared similar cardenolide traits, but not glucosinolate traits, likely as a result of specific selective pressures acting on each defense. Ancestral and novel chemical defenses in <i>Erysimum</i> thus appear to provide complementary rather than redundant functions.</p>




  </div>

</section>


                
                    <section
    class="article-section "
   id="digest"
  data-behaviour="ArticleSection"
  
>

  <header class="article-section__header">
    <h2 class="article-section__header_text">eLife digest</h2>
  </header>

  <div class="article-section__body">
      <p class="paragraph">Plants are often attacked by insects and other herbivores. As a result, they have evolved to defend themselves by producing many different chemicals that are toxic to these pests. As producing each chemical costs energy, individual plants often only produce one type of chemical that is targeted towards their main herbivore. Related species of plants often use the same type of chemical defense so, if a particular herbivore gains the ability to cope with this chemical, it may rapidly become an important pest for the whole plant family.</p>
<p class="paragraph">To escape this threat, some plants have gained the ability to produce more than one type of chemical defense. Wallflowers, for example, are a group of plants in the mustard family that produce two types of toxic chemicals: mustard oils, which are common in most plants in this family; and cardenolides, which are an innovation of the wallflowers, and which are otherwise found only in distantly related plants such as foxglove and milkweed. The combination of these two chemical defenses within the same plant may have allowed the wallflowers to escape attacks from their main herbivores and may explain why the number of wallflower species rapidly increased within the last two million years.</p>
<p class="paragraph">Zst et al. have now studied the diversity of mustard oils and cardenolides present in many different species of wallflower. This analysis revealed that almost all of the tested wallflower species produced high amounts of both chemical defenses, while only one species lacked the ability to produce cardenolides. The levels of mustard oils had no relation to the levels of cardenolides in the tested species, which suggests that the regulation of these two defenses is not linked. Furthermore, Zst et al. found that closely related wallflower species produced more similar cardenolides, but less similar mustard oils, to each other. This suggests that mustard oils and cardenolides have evolved independently in wallflowers and have distinct roles in the defense against different herbivores.</p>
<p class="paragraph">The evolution of insect resistance to pesticides and other toxins is an important concern for agriculture. Applying multiple toxins to crops at the same time is an important strategy to slow the evolution of resistance in the pests. The findings of Zst et al. describe a system in which plants have naturally evolved an equivalent strategy to escape their main herbivores. Understanding how plants produce multiple chemical defenses, and the costs involved, may help efforts to breed crop species that are more resistant to herbivores and require fewer applications of pesticides.</p>




  </div>

</section>


                
                    <section
    class="article-section "
   id="s1"
  data-behaviour="ArticleSection"
  
>

  <header class="article-section__header">
    <h2 class="article-section__header_text">Introduction</h2>
  </header>

  <div class="article-section__body">
      <p class="paragraph">Plant chemical defenses play a central role in the coevolutionary arms race with herbivorous insects. In response to diverse environmental challenges, plants have evolved a plethora of structurally diverse organic compounds with repellent, antinutritive, or toxic properties (<a href="#bib52">Fraenkel, 1959</a>; <a href="#bib120">Mithfer and Boland, 2012</a>). Chemical defenses can impose barriers to consumption by herbivores, but in parallel may favor the evolution of specialized herbivores that can tolerate or disable these defenses (<a href="#bib37">Cornell and Hawkins, 2003</a>). Chemical diversity is likely evolving in response to a multitude of plant-herbivore interactions (<a href="#bib156">Salazar et al., 2018</a>), and community-level phytochemical diversity may be a key driver of niche segregation and insect community dynamics (<a href="#bib151">Richards et al., 2015</a>; <a href="#bib160">Sedio et al., 2017</a>).</p>
<p class="paragraph">For individual plants, the production of diverse mixtures of chemicals is often considered advantageous (<a href="#bib153">Romeo, 1996</a>; <a href="#bib50">Firn and Jones, 2003</a>; <a href="#bib56">Gershenzon et al., 2012</a>; <a href="#bib51">Forbey et al., 2013</a>; <a href="#bib152">Richards et al., 2016</a>). For example, different chemicals may target distinct herbivores (<a href="#bib79">Iason et al., 2011</a>; <a href="#bib151">Richards et al., 2015</a>), or may act synergistically to increase overall toxicity of a plant (<a href="#bib168">Steppuhn and Baldwin, 2007</a>). However, metabolic constraints can limit the extent of phytochemical diversity within individual plants (<a href="#bib50">Firn and Jones, 2003</a>). Most defensive metabolites originate from a small group of precursor compounds and conserved biosynthetic pathways, which are modified in a hierarchical process into diverse, species-specific end products (<a href="#bib122">Moore et al., 2014</a>). As constraints are likely strongest for the early stages of these pathways, related plant species commonly share the same functional classes of defensive chemicals (<a href="#bib183">Wink, 2003</a>), but vary considerably in the number of compounds within each class (<a href="#bib47">Fahey et al., 2001</a>; <a href="#bib147">Rasmann and Agrawal, 2011</a>).</p>
<p class="paragraph">Functional conservatism in defensive chemicals among related plants should facilitate host expansion and the evolution of tolerance in herbivores (<a href="#bib37">Cornell and Hawkins, 2003</a>), as specialized resistance mechanisms against one type of compound are more likely to be effective against structurally similar than structurally dissimilar compounds. This may result in a seemingly paradoxical scenario, wherein well-defended plants are nonetheless attacked by a diverse community of specialized herbivores (<a href="#bib3">Agrawal, 2005</a>; <a href="#bib15">Bidart-Bouzat and Kliebenstein, 2008</a>). For example, most plants in the Brassicaceae produce glucosinolates as their primary defense, which upon activation by myrosinase (thioglucoside glucohydrolase) enzymes upon leaf damage become potent repellents of many herbivores (<a href="#bib47">Fahey et al., 2001</a>). However, despite the potency of this defense system and the large diversity of glucosinolates produced by the Brassicaceae, several specialized herbivores have evolved strategies to overcome this defense, enabling them to consume most Brassicaceae and even to sequester glucosinolates for their own defense against predators (<a href="#bib123">Mller, 2009</a>; <a href="#bib182">Winde and Wittstock, 2011</a>).</p>
<p class="paragraph">Plants may occasionally overcome the constraints on functional diversification and gain the ability to produce new classes of defensive chemicals as a second line of defense (<a href="#bib49">Feeny, 1977</a>). Although this phenomenon is likely widespread across the plant kingdom, it has most commonly been reported from the well-studied Brassicaceae. In addition to producing evolutionarily ancestral glucosinolates, plants in this family have gained the ability to produce saponins in <i>Barbarea vulgaris</i> (<a href="#bib162">Shinoda et al., 2002</a>), alkaloids in <i>Cochlearia officinalis</i> (<a href="#bib20">Brock et al., 2006</a>), cucurbitacins in <i>Iberis</i> spp. (<a href="#bib129">Nielsen, 1978b</a>), alliarinoside in <i>Alliaria petiolata</i> (<a href="#bib54">Frisch and Mller, 2012</a>), and cardenolides in the genus <i>Erysimum</i> (<a href="#bib112">Makarevich et al., 1994</a>). These recently-evolved chemical defenses with modes of action distinct from glucosinolates have likely allowed the plants to escape attack from specialized, glucosinolate-adapted herbivores (<a href="#bib129">Nielsen, 1978b</a>; <a href="#bib38">Dimock et al., 1991</a>; <a href="#bib69">Haribal and Renwick, 2001</a>; <a href="#bib162">Shinoda et al., 2002</a>). Gains of novel defenses are expected to result in a release from selective pressures imposed by specialized antagonists, and thus may represent key steps in herbivore-plant coevolution that lead to rapid phylogenetic diversification (<a href="#bib180">Weber and Agrawal, 2014</a>).</p>
<p class="paragraph">The production of cardenolides by species in the genus <i>Erysimum</i> is one of the longest- and best-studied examples of an evolutionarily recent gain of a novel chemical defense (<a href="#bib81">Jaretzky and Wilcke, 1932</a>; <a href="#bib125">Nagata et al., 1957</a>; <a href="#bib163">Singh and Rastogi, 1970</a>; <a href="#bib112">Makarevich et al., 1994</a>). Cardenolides are a type of cardiac glycoside, which act as allosteric inhibitors of Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase, an essential membrane ion transporter that is expressed ubiquitously in animal cells (<a href="#bib4">Agrawal et al., 2012</a>). Cardiac glycosides are produced by plants in approximately sixty genera belonging to twelve plant families, and several cardiac glycoside-producing plants are known for their toxicity or medicinal uses (<a href="#bib4">Agrawal et al., 2012</a>; <a href="#bib192">Zst et al., 2018</a>). <i>Erysimum</i> is a species-rich genus consisting of diploid and polyploid species with diverse morphologies, growth habits, and ecological niches (<a href="#bib6">Al-Shehbaz, 1988</a>; <a href="#bib143">Polatschek and Snogerup, 2002</a>; <a href="#bib7">Al-Shehbaz, 2010</a>; <a href="#bib62">Gmez et al., 2015</a>). Of the <i>Erysimum</i> species evaluated to date, all produce some of the novel cardenolide defenses (<a href="#bib112">Makarevich et al., 1994</a>). Previous phylogenetic studies suggest a recent and rapid diversification of the genus, with most species divergence occurring within the last 23 million years (<a href="#bib61">Gmez et al., 2014</a>; <a href="#bib121">Moazzeni et al., 2014</a>), resulting in 150 to 350 extant species (<a href="#bib143">Polatschek and Snogerup, 2002</a>; <a href="#bib7">Al-Shehbaz, 2010</a>). The large uncertainty in species number reflects taxonomic challenges in this genus, which includes many species that readily hybridize, as well as cryptic species with near-identical morphology (<a href="#bib1">Abdelaziz et al., 2011</a>).</p>
<p class="paragraph">In most <i>Erysimum</i> species, cardenolides appear to have enabled an escape from at least some glucosinolate-adapted specialist herbivores. Cardenolides in <i>Erysimum</i> act as oviposition and feeding deterrents for different pierid butterflies (<a href="#bib30">Chew, 1975</a>; <a href="#bib31">Chew, 1977</a>; <a href="#bib181">Wiklund et al., 1978</a>; <a href="#bib149">Renwick et al., 1989</a>; <a href="#bib38">Dimock et al., 1991</a>), and several glucosinolate-adapted beetles (<i>Phaedon</i> spp. and <i>Phyllotreta</i> spp.) were deterred from feeding by dietary cardenolides at levels commonly found in <i>Erysimum</i> (<a href="#bib128">Nielsen, 1978a</a>; <a href="#bib129">Nielsen, 1978b</a>). Nonetheless, <i>Erysimum</i> plants are still attacked by a range of herbivores and seed predators, including some mammals and several glucosinolate-adapted aphids, true bugs, and lepidopteran larvae (<a href="#bib60">Gmez, 2005</a>; <a href="#bib192">Zst et al., 2018</a>). These herbivores likely rely on general detoxification mechanisms for tolerance of the novel defense, while to date there are no reports of de novo gains of specialized cardenolide resistance in <i>Erysimum</i> herbivores. However, the gain of the novel defense may have facilitated host shifts in at least one cardenolide-adapted herbivore: in addition to its main host <i>Digitalis purpurea</i> (Plantaginaceae), the seed-feeding bug <i>Horvathiolus superbus</i> (Lygaeinae) commonly feeds on seeds of <i>E. crepidifolium</i>, and is able to sequester cardenolides from both sources (Georg Petschenka, personal observations).</p>
<p class="paragraph">The gain of a novel chemical defense makes the genus <i>Erysimum</i> an excellent model system to study the causes and consequences of phytochemical diversification (<a href="#bib192">Zst et al., 2018</a>). While an increasing number of studies are beginning to describe taxon-wide patterns of chemical diversity in plants (e.g., <a href="#bib151">Richards et al., 2015</a>; <a href="#bib160">Sedio et al., 2017</a>; <a href="#bib156">Salazar et al., 2018</a>), the <i>Erysimum</i> system is unique in combining two classes of plant metabolites with primarily defensive function  although a broader role of glucosinolates is increasingly recognized (e.g., <a href="#bib86">Katz et al., 2015</a>). The system thus is ideally suited to evaluate the evolutionary consequences of co-expressing two functionally distinct but potentially redundant defenses. Here, we present a high-quality genome sequence assembly and annotation for the short-lived annual <i>E. cheiranthoides</i> as an important resource for future molecular studies in this system. Furthermore, we present a new phylogeny for 48 species constructed from transcriptome sequences (<a href="#fig1">Figure 1</a>), corresponding to 1030% of species in the genus <i>Erysimum</i>. We combine this phylogeny with a characterization of the full diversity of glucosinolates and cardenolides in leaves to evaluate macroevolutionary patterns in the evolution of phytochemical diversity across the genus. We complemented the characterization of defensive phenotypes by quantifying glucosinolate-activating myrosinase activity, inhibition of animal Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase by leaf extracts, and defense inducibility in response to exogenous application of jasmonic acid (JA). By assessing co-variation of diversity, abundance and inducibility of ancestral and novel defenses, we provide evidence that these two classes of defense metabolites evolved in response to different selective pressures and appear to have specific, non-redundant functions.</p>

                    <section
    class="article-section "
   id="s2"
  data-behaviour="ArticleSection"
  data-initial-state="closed"
>

  <header class="article-section__header">
    <h2 class="article-section__header_text">Results</h2>
  </header>

  <div class="article-section__body">
      <section
    class="article-section "
   id="s2-1"
  
  
>

  <header class="article-section__header">
    <h3 class="article-section__header_text"><i>E.cheiranthoides</i> genome assembly</h3>
  </header>

  <div class="article-section__body">
      <p class="paragraph">A total of 39.5 Gb of PacBio sequences with an average read length of 10,603 bp were assembled into 1087 contigs with an N50 of 1.5 Mbp (<a href="#table1">Table 1</a>). Hi-C scaffolding oriented 98.5% of the assembly into eight large scaffolds representing pseudomolecules (<a href="#table1">Table 1</a>, <a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig2s1">Figure 2figure supplement 1</a>), while 216 small contigs remained unanchored. The final assembly (v1.2) had a total length of 174.5 Mbp, representing 86% of the estimated genome size of <i>E. cheiranthoides</i> and capturing 99% of the BUSCO gene set (<a href="#table1">Table 1</a>, <a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig2s2">Figure 2figure supplement 2</a>). Sequences were deposited under GenBank project ID PRJNA563696 and additionally are provided at <a href="http://www.erysimum.org">www.erysimum.org</a>. A total of 29,947 gene models were predicted and captured 98% of the BUSCO gene set (<a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig2s3">Figure 2figure supplement 3</a>). In the presumed centromere regions of each chromosome, genic sequences were less abundant, whereas repeat sequences were more common (<a href="#fig2">Figure 2A</a>). Repetitive sequences constituted approximately 29% of the genome (<a href="/articles/51712/figures#supp2">Supplementary file 2</a>). Long terminal repeat retrotransposons (LTR-RT) made up the largest proportion of the repeats identified (<a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig2s4">Figure 2figure supplement 4</a>). Among these, repeats in the <i>Gypsy</i> superfamily constituted the largest fraction of the genome (<a href="/articles/51712/figures#supp2">Supplementary file 2</a>). The majority of the LTR elements appeared to be relatively young, with most having estimated insertion times of less than 1 MYA (<a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig2s5">Figure 2figure supplement 5</a>). Synteny analysis showed evidence of several chromosomal fusions and fissions between the eight chromosomes of <i>E. cheiranthoides</i> and the five chromosomes of Arabidopsis (<a href="#fig2">Figure 2B</a>).</p>
    <div
        id="table1"
        class="asset-viewer-inline asset-viewer-inline--table "
        data-variant="table"
        data-behaviour="AssetNavigation AssetViewer ToggleableCaption"
        data-selector=".caption-text__body"
        data-asset-viewer-group="table1"
    >
    
      <div class="asset-viewer-inline__header_panel">
          <div class="asset-viewer-inline__header_text">
            <span class="asset-viewer-inline__header_text__prominent">Table 1</span>
          </div>
    
    
            <div class="asset-viewer-inline__figure_access">
            </div>
    
      </div>
    
          <figure class="captioned-asset">
          
          
          
          
          
          
              <figcaption class="captioned-asset__caption">
          
                  <h6 class="caption-text__heading">Assembly metrics for the <i>E. cheiranthoides</i> genome: v0.9=Falcon +Arrow assembly results, v1.2=genome assembly after Hi-C scaffolding and Pilon correction.</h6>
                
                

          
          
              </figcaption>
          
          
          
              <div class="table">
              
              
                  <div class="table__table">
              
                    <table><thead><tr><th valign="top"/><th valign="top">v0.9</th><th valign="top">v1.2 pseudomolecules and contigs</th><th valign="top">v1.2 pseudomolecules only</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td valign="top">total length (Mbp)</td><td valign="top">177.4</td><td valign="top">177.2</td><td valign="top">174.5</td></tr><tr><td valign="top">expected size (Mbp)</td><td valign="top">205</td><td valign="top">205</td><td valign="top">205</td></tr><tr><td valign="top">number of contigs</td><td valign="top">1087</td><td valign="top">224</td><td valign="top">8</td></tr><tr><td valign="top">N50 (Mbp)</td><td valign="top">1.5</td><td valign="top">22.4</td><td valign="top">22.4</td></tr><tr><td valign="top">complete BUSCOs (out of 1,375)</td><td valign="top">1359</td><td valign="top">1346</td><td valign="top">1356</td></tr><tr><td valign="top">complete and single copy BUSCOs (out of 1,375)</td><td valign="top">1271</td><td valign="top">1300</td><td valign="top">1306</td></tr><tr><td valign="top">complete and duplicated BUSCOs (out of 1,375)</td><td valign="top">88</td><td valign="top">46</td><td valign="top">50</td></tr><tr><td valign="top">fragmented BUSCOs (out of 1,375)</td><td valign="top">5</td><td valign="top">8</td><td valign="top">6</td></tr><tr><td valign="top">missing BUSCOs (out of 1,375)</td><td valign="top">11</td><td valign="top">21</td><td valign="top">13</td></tr></tbody></table>

<section
    class="article-section "
   id="s2-2"
  
  
>

  <header class="article-section__header">
    <h3 class="article-section__header_text">Glucosinolate and myrosinase genes in the <i>E. cheiranthoides</i> genome</h3>
  </header>

  <div class="article-section__body">
      <p class="paragraph">Three aliphatic glucosinolates  glucoiberverin (3-methylthiopropyl glucosinolate), glucoiberin (3-methylsulfinylpropyl glucosinolate), and glucocheirolin (3-methylsulfonylpropyl glucosinolate)  have been reported as the main glucosinolates in <i>E. cheiranthoides</i> (<a href="#bib35">Cole, 1976</a>; <a href="#bib76">Huang et al., 1993</a>). We confirmed their dominance in <i>E. cheiranthoides</i> var. <i>Elbtalaue</i>, but also identified additional aliphatic and indole glucosinolates at lower concentrations. By making use of the known glucosinolate biosynthetic genes from Arabidopsis (<a href="#bib78">Hull et al., 2000</a>; <a href="#bib116">Mikkelsen et al., 2000</a>; <a href="#bib12">Bak and Feyereisen, 2001</a>; <a href="#bib11">Bak et al., 2001</a>; <a href="#bib96">Kliebenstein et al., 2001b</a>; <a href="#bib103">Kroymann et al., 2001</a>; <a href="#bib148">Reintanz et al., 2001</a>; <a href="#bib28">Chen et al., 2003</a>; <a href="#bib104">Kroymann et al., 2003</a>; <a href="#bib127">Naur et al., 2003</a>; <a href="#bib64">Grubb et al., 2004</a>; <a href="#bib117">Mikkelsen et al., 2004</a>; <a href="#bib141">Piotrowski et al., 2004</a>; <a href="#bib171">Textor et al., 2004</a>; <a href="#bib131">Nozawa et al., 2005</a>; <a href="#bib93">Klein et al., 2006</a>; <a href="#bib159">Schuster et al., 2006</a>; <a href="#bib67">Hansen et al., 2007</a>; <a href="#bib172">Textor et al., 2007</a>; <a href="#bib68">Hansen et al., 2008</a>; <a href="#bib97">Knill et al., 2008</a>; <a href="#bib110">Li et al., 2008</a>; <a href="#bib48">Farquharson, 2009</a>; <a href="#bib57">Geu-Flores et al., 2009</a>; <a href="#bib59">Gigolashvili et al., 2009</a>; <a href="#bib72">He et al., 2009</a>; <a href="#bib94">Klein and Papenbrock, 2009</a>; <a href="#bib98">Knill et al., 2009</a>; <a href="#bib138">Pfalz et al., 2009</a>; <a href="#bib157">Sawada et al., 2009</a>; <a href="#bib73">He et al., 2010</a>; <a href="#bib58">Geu-Flores et al., 2011</a>; <a href="#bib74">He et al., 2011</a>; <a href="#bib139">Pfalz et al., 2011</a>; <a href="#bib108">Lchler et al., 2015</a>; <a href="#bib99">Kong et al., 2016</a>; <a href="#bib140">Pfalz et al., 2016</a>), BLASTn comparisons to the <i>E. cheiranthoides</i> genome, and creating phylogenetic trees to compare nucleotide coding sequences of Arabidopsis, <i>Brassica</i>, and <i>E. cheiranthoides</i>, we identified homologs of genes encoding both indole (<a href="#fig3">Figure 3</a>, <a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig3s1">Figure 3figure supplements 1</a><a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig3s7">7</a>) and aliphatic (<a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig4s1">Figure 4figure supplements 1</a><a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig4s8">8</a>) glucosinolate biosynthetic enzymes.</p>
    <div
        id="fig3"
        class="asset-viewer-inline asset-viewer-inline-- "
        data-variant=""
        data-behaviour="AssetNavigation AssetViewer ToggleableCaption"
        data-selector=".caption-text__body"
        data-asset-viewer-group="fig3"
        data-asset-viewer-uri="https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:51712%2Felife-51712-fig3-v2.tif/full/,1500/0/default.jpg"
        data-asset-viewer-width="1074"
        data-asset-viewer-height="1500"
    >
    
      <div class="asset-viewer-inline__header_panel">
          <div class="asset-viewer-inline__header_text">
            <span class="asset-viewer-inline__header_text__prominent">Figure 3</span> with 7 supplements <a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig3" class="asset-viewer-inline__header_link">see all</a>
          </div>
    
    
            <div class="asset-viewer-inline__figure_access">
              <a href="https://elifesciences.org/download/aHR0cHM6Ly9paWlmLmVsaWZlc2NpZW5jZXMub3JnL2xheDo1MTcxMiUyRmVsaWZlLTUxNzEyLWZpZzMtdjIudGlmL2Z1bGwvZnVsbC8wL2RlZmF1bHQuanBn/elife-51712-fig3-v2.jpg?_hash=dKkuQZcbOJ4CA2hAXdyLkyZSkd6AfDTGY2BNJHlZr2Y%3D" class="asset-viewer-inline__download_all_link" download="Download"><span class="visuallyhidden">Download asset</span></a>
              <a href="https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:51712%2Felife-51712-fig3-v2.tif/full/,1500/0/default.jpg" class="asset-viewer-inline__open_link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"><span class="visuallyhidden">Open asset</span></a>
            </div>
    
      </div>
    
          <figure class="captioned-asset">
          
              <a href="https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:51712%2Felife-51712-fig3-v2.tif/full/,1500/0/default.jpg" class="captioned-asset__link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">
              <picture class="captioned-asset__picture">
                  <source srcset="https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:51712%2Felife-51712-fig3-v2.tif/full/1234,/0/default.webp 2x, https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:51712%2Felife-51712-fig3-v2.tif/full/617,/0/default.webp 1x"
                      type="image/webp"
                      >
                  <source srcset="https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:51712%2Felife-51712-fig3-v2.tif/full/1234,/0/default.jpg 2x, https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:51712%2Felife-51712-fig3-v2.tif/full/617,/0/default.jpg 1x"
                      type="image/jpeg"
                      >
                  <img src="https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:51712%2Felife-51712-fig3-v2.tif/full/617,/0/default.jpg"
                       
                       alt=""
                       class="captioned-asset__image"
                  >
              </picture>
              </a>
          
          
          
          
          
              <figcaption class="captioned-asset__caption">
          
                  <h6 class="caption-text__heading">Identification of known indole glucosinolate biosynthetic genes and glucosinolate-modifying genes from Arabidopsis in <i>Erysimum cheiranthoides</i>.</h6>
                
                
                <div class="caption-text__body"><p class="paragraph">(<b>A</b>) Starting with tryptophan, indole glucosinolates are synthesized using some enzymes that also function in aliphatic glucosinolate biosynthesis (GGP1; SUR1; UGT74B1) while also using indole glucosinolate-specific enzymes. (<b>B</b>) Indole glucosinolates can be modified by hydroxylation and subsequent methylation. Red square brackets indicate where gene copy numbers differ between Arabidopsis and <i>E. cheiranthoides</i>. Glucosinolates with names highlighted in blue were identified in <i>Erysimum cheiranthoides</i> var. <i>Elbtalaue</i>. Abbreviations: cytochrome P450 monooxygenase (CYP); glutathione <i>S</i>-transferase F (GSTF); glutathione (GSH); -glutamyl peptidase 1 (GGP1); SUPERROOT 1 C-S lyase (SUR1); UDP-dependent glycosyltransferase (UGT); sulfotransferase (SOT); glucosinolate (GS); indole glucosinolate methyltransferase (IGMT).</p>
</div>
          
          
              </figcaption>
          
          
          
          
          </figure>
    
    
    </div>
<p class="paragraph">Homologs of all genes of the biosynthetic pathway for glucobrassicin (indol-3-ylmethyl glucosinolate) and its 4-hydroxy and 4-methoxy derivatives were present in <i>E. cheiranthoides</i> (<a href="#fig3">Figure 3</a>). Consistent with the absence of neoglucobrassicin (1-methoxy-indol-3-ylmethyl glucosinolate) in <i>E. cheiranthoides</i> var. <i>Elbtalaue</i>, we did not find homologs of the Arabidopsis genes encoding the biosynthesis of this compound.</p>
<p class="paragraph">Genes encoding the complete biosynthetic pathway of the <i>E. cheiranthoides</i> aliphatic glucosinolates glucoiberverin, glucoiberin, glucoerucin (4-methylthiobutyl glucosinolate), and glucoraphanin (4-methylsulfinylbutyl glucosinolate) were present in the genome (<a href="#fig4">Figure 4</a>, <a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig4s1">Figure 4figure supplements 1</a><a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig4s6">6</a>). Because the <i>E. cheiranthoides</i> methylsulfonyl glucosinolates glucocheirolin, glucoerysolin (4-methylsulfonylbutyl glucosinolate), and 3-hydroxy-4-methylsulfonylbutyl glucosinolate are not present in Arabidopsis, genes encoding their biosynthesis are unknown and could not be identified as part of this study. The <i>E. cheiranthoides</i> genome contains genes with similarity to Arabidopsis <i>ALKENYL HYDROXALKYL PRODUCING</i> (<i>AOP2</i> and <i>AOP3</i>), and <i>3-BUTENYL GLUCOSINOLATE 2-HYDROXYLASE</i> (<i>GS-OH</i>) (<a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig4s7">Figure 4figure supplements 7</a> and <a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig4s8">8</a>). However, the apparent absence of sinigrin (2-propenyl glucosinolate), 2-hydroxypropyl glucosinolate, progoitrin (2-hydroxy-3-butenyl glucosinolate), and 4-hydroxybutyl glucosinolate in <i>E. cheiranthoides</i> (<a href="#fig4">Figure 4</a>), suggests that the encoded enzymes of these genes have other functions. <i>CYP79A2</i>, which functions in the biosynthesis of benzyl glucosinolates that are present in very small amounts in seeds of Arabidopsis ecotype Columbia (<a href="#bib184">Wittstock and Halkier, 2000</a>), has a homolog in the <i>E. cheiranthoides</i> genome (<a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig3s1">Figure 3figure supplement 1</a>). Although we did not observe benzyl glucosinolates in <i>E. cheiranthoides</i>, this lack of detection could be due to assay sensitivity or not testing all tissue types. Homologs of the Arabidopsis <i>CYP79C1</i> and <i>CYP79C2</i> genes, which have unknown functions but are hypothesized to be involved in glucosinolate biosynthesis (<a href="#bib66">Halkier and Gershenzon, 2006</a>), are present in the <i>E. cheiranthoides</i> genome (<a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig3s1">Figure 3figure supplement 1</a>). <i>CYP79D2</i> from cassava catalyzed the formation of valine- and isoleucine-derived glucosinolates in Arabidopsis (<a href="#bib118">Mikkelsen and Halkier, 2003</a>), yet no <i>CYP79D</i> genes appear to be present in <i>E. cheiranthoides</i> (<a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig3s1">Figure 3figure supplement 1</a>). Additionally, there was no clear <i>E. cheiranthoides</i> homolog of <i>GLUCORAPHASATIN SYNTHASE 1</i> (<i>GRS1</i>, <a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig4s9">Figure 4figure supplement 9</a>), a 2-oxoglutarate-dependent dioxygenase that contributes to glucoraphasatin (4-methylthio-3-butenyl glucosinolate) biosynthesis in <i>R. sativus</i> (<a href="#bib84">Kakizaki et al., 2017</a>).</p>
    <div
        id="fig4"
        class="asset-viewer-inline asset-viewer-inline-- "
        data-variant=""
        data-behaviour="AssetNavigation AssetViewer ToggleableCaption"
        data-selector=".caption-text__body"
        data-asset-viewer-group="fig4"
        data-asset-viewer-uri="https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:51712%2Felife-51712-fig4-v2.tif/full/1500,/0/default.jpg"
        data-asset-viewer-width="1500"
        data-asset-viewer-height="1470"
    >
    
      <div class="asset-viewer-inline__header_panel">
          <div class="asset-viewer-inline__header_text">
            <span class="asset-viewer-inline__header_text__prominent">Figure 4</span> with 12 supplements <a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig4" class="asset-viewer-inline__header_link">see all</a>
          </div>
    
    
            <div class="asset-viewer-inline__figure_access">
              <a href="https://elifesciences.org/download/aHR0cHM6Ly9paWlmLmVsaWZlc2NpZW5jZXMub3JnL2xheDo1MTcxMiUyRmVsaWZlLTUxNzEyLWZpZzQtdjIudGlmL2Z1bGwvZnVsbC8wL2RlZmF1bHQuanBn/elife-51712-fig4-v2.jpg?_hash=%2BL47T8HJTbuPF3WUJADDe%2FSJnF%2B3F2hre71oAMoCMyk%3D" class="asset-viewer-inline__download_all_link" download="Download"><span class="visuallyhidden">Download asset</span></a>
              <a href="https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:51712%2Felife-51712-fig4-v2.tif/full/1500,/0/default.jpg" class="asset-viewer-inline__open_link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer"><span class="visuallyhidden">Open asset</span></a>
            </div>
    
      </div>
    
          <figure class="captioned-asset">
          
              <a href="https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:51712%2Felife-51712-fig4-v2.tif/full/1500,/0/default.jpg" class="captioned-asset__link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">
              <picture class="captioned-asset__picture">
                  <source srcset="https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:51712%2Felife-51712-fig4-v2.tif/full/1234,/0/default.webp 2x, https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:51712%2Felife-51712-fig4-v2.tif/full/617,/0/default.webp 1x"
                      type="image/webp"
                      >
                  <source srcset="https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:51712%2Felife-51712-fig4-v2.tif/full/1234,/0/default.jpg 2x, https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:51712%2Felife-51712-fig4-v2.tif/full/617,/0/default.jpg 1x"
                      type="image/jpeg"
                      >
                  <img src="https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:51712%2Felife-51712-fig4-v2.tif/full/617,/0/default.jpg"
                       
                       alt=""
                       class="captioned-asset__image"
                  >
              </picture>
              </a>
          
          
          
          
          
              <figcaption class="captioned-asset__caption">
          
                  <h6 class="caption-text__heading">Identification of known aliphatic glucosinolate biosynthetic genesandglucosinolate-modifying genes from Arabidopsis in <i>Erysimum cheiranthoides.</i></h6>
                
                
                <div class="caption-text__body"><p class="paragraph">Aliphatic glucosinolates are synthesized from methionine by a series of enzymes, while additional enzymes are responsible for aliphatic glucosinolate modifications (black box).Red square brackets indicate where gene copy numbers differ between Arabidopsis and <i>E. cheiranthoides</i>, or where gene copies could not be matched unambiguously between species. Glucosinolates with names highlighted in blue were identified in <i>Erysimum cheiranthoides</i> var. <i>Elbtalaue.</i> Abbreviations: branched-chain aminotransferase (BCAT); bile acid transporter (BAT); methylthioalkylmalate synthase (MAM); isopropylmalate isomerase (IPMI); large subunit (LSU); small subunit (SSU); isopropylmalate dehydrogenase(IPMDH); cytochrome P450 monooxygenase (CYP); glutathione <i>S</i>-transferase F (GSTF); glutathione <i>S</i>-transferase Tau (GSTU); glutathione (GSH); -glutamyl peptidase 1 (GGP1); SUPERROOT 1 C-S lyase (SUR1); UDP-dependent glycosyltransferase (UGT); sulfotransferase (SOT); flavin monooxygenase (FMO); glucosinolate oxoglutarate-dependent dioxygenase (AOP); 3-butenyl glucosinolate 2-hydroxylase (GS-OH).</p>
</div>
          
          
              </figcaption>
          
          
          
          
          </figure>
    
    
    </div>
<p class="paragraph">In response to insect feeding or pathogen infection, glucosinolates are activated by myrosinase enzymes (<a href="#bib66">Halkier and Gershenzon, 2006</a>). Between-gene phylogenetic comparisons revealed that homologs of known Arabidopsis myrosinases, the main foliar myrosinases <i>TGG1</i> and <i>TGG2</i> (<a href="#bib187">Xue et al., 1995</a>; <a href="#bib13">Barth and Jander, 2006</a>), root-expressed <i>TGG4</i> and <i>TGG5</i> (<a href="#bib9">Andersson et al., 2009</a>), and likely pseudogenes <i>TGG3</i> and <i>TGG6</i> (<a href="#bib145">Rask et al., 2000</a>; <a href="#bib188">Zhang et al., 2002</a>), were also present in the <i>E. cheiranthoides</i> genome (<a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig4s10">Figure 4figure supplement 10</a>). Additionally, we found homologs of the more distantly related Arabidopsis myrosinases <i>PEN2</i> (<a href="#bib14">Bednarek et al., 2009</a>; <a href="#bib34">Clay et al., 2009</a>) and <i>PYK10</i> (<a href="#bib161">Sherameti et al., 2008</a>; <a href="#bib126">Nakano et al., 2017</a>). In Arabidopsis, protein products of <i>epithiospecifier protein</i> (<i>ESP</i>), <i>epithiospecifier modifier</i> (<i>ESM</i>), and <i>nitrile specifier protein</i> (<i>NSP</i>) direct glucosinolate breakdown into nitriles, thiocyanates, or isothiocyanates (<a href="#bib109">Lambrix et al., 2001</a>; <a href="#bib21">Burow et al., 2006</a>; <a href="#bib189">Zhang et al., 2006</a>). Although we did not measure glucosinolate breakdown in <i>Erysimum</i>, we did find <i>ESP, ESM</i>, and <i>NSP</i> homologs in the <i>E. cheiranthoides</i> genome (<a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig4s11">Figure 4figure supplements 11</a> and <a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig4s12">12</a>). Therefore, the pathway of glucosinolate activation appears to be largely conserved between Arabidopsis and <i>E. cheiranthoides</i>.</p>




  </div>

</section>
<section
    class="article-section "
   id="s2-3"
  
  
>

  <header class="article-section__header">
    <h3 class="article-section__header_text">Phylogenetic relationship of 48 <i>Erysimum</i> species</h3>
  </header>

  <div class="article-section__body">
      <p class="paragraph">Assemblies of transcriptomes from 48 <i>Erysimum</i> species (including <i>E. cheiranthoides</i>) had N50 values ranging from 574 to 2,160 bp (<a href="/articles/51712/figures#supp3">Supplementary file 3</a>). Transcriptome assemblies contained completed genes from 5494% of the BUSCO set and coding sequence lengths were generally shorter on average than the <i>E. cheiranthoides</i> coding sequence lengths (<a href="/articles/51712/figures#supp3">Supplementary file 3</a>). Transcriptome sequences were deposited under GenBank project ID PRJNA563696 and at <a href="http://www.erysimum.org">www.erysimum.org</a>. The large number of orthologous gene sequences identified among the <i>E. cheiranthoides</i> genome and the 48 transcriptomes resulted in an ASTRAL species tree with high posterior probabilities for most nodes (<a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig5s1">Figure 5figure supplement 1</a>, <a href="/articles/51712/figures#supp4">Supplementary file 4</a>). To determine divergence times among the 48 species, we generated a chronogram using a concatenated ExaML species tree with branch length information (<a href="#fig5">Figure 5</a>). While we relied on published estimates to constrain ages of several internal nodes, our analysis aligns well with a recent, rapid radiation of the species included in our study within the last 24 Mya (<a href="#fig5">Figure 5</a>).</p>
    <div
        id="fig5"
        class="asset-viewer-inline asset-viewer-inline-- "
        data-variant=""
        data-behaviour="AssetNavigation AssetViewer ToggleableCaption"
        data-selector=".caption-text__body"
        data-asset-viewer-group="fig5"
        data-asset-viewer-uri="https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:51712%2Felife-51712-fig5-v2.tif/full/1500,/0/default.jpg"
        data-asset-viewer-width="1500"
        data-asset-viewer-height="1331"

      <div class="asset-viewer-inline__header_panel">

              <a href="https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:51712%2Felife-51712-fig5-v2.tif/full/1500,/0/default.jpg" class="captioned-asset__link" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">
              <picture class="captioned-asset__picture">
                  <source srcset="https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:51712%2Felife-51712-fig5-v2.tif/full/1234,/0/default.webp 2x, https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:51712%2Felife-51712-fig5-v2.tif/full/617,/0/default.webp 1x"

                      >
                  <source srcset="https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:51712%2Felife-51712-fig5-v2.tif/full/1234,/0/default.jpg 2x, https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:51712%2Felife-51712-fig5-v2.tif/full/617,/0/default.jpg 1x"
                      type="image/jpeg"
                      >
                  <img src="https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:51712%2Felife-51712-fig5-v2.tif/full/617,/0/default.jpg"
                       
                       alt=""
                       class="captioned-asset__image"
                  >
              </picture>
              </a>
          
          
          
          
          
              <figcaption class="captioned-asset__caption">
          
                  <h6 class="caption-text__heading">Genome-guided concatenated phylogeny of 48 <i>Erysimum</i> species.</h6>
                
                
                <div class="caption-text__body"><p class="paragraph">Phylogenetic relationships were inferred from 9868 orthologous genes using ExaML with <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> as outgroup. Node depth corresponds to divergence time in million years. Pie charts on each internode show concordance factors, with gray segments corresponding to the proportion of gene tree supporting the main topology. Nodes are labelled as 1 to 47; see <a href="/articles/51712/figures#supp5">Supplementary file 5</a> for concordance factor values and number of decisive trees of each node. Four nodes were constrained using published divergence time estimates, with the range of constraints indicated by gray bars. Known polyploid species are highlighted in red. Approximate geographic range of species is provided in parentheses. The horticultural species <i>E. cheiri</i> and the weedy species <i>E. cheiranthoides</i> and <i>E. repandum</i> are of European origin but are now widespread across the Northern Hemisphere. Clades of species from shared geographic origins are highlighted in different colors. On the right, pictures of rosettes of a representative subset of species is provided to highlight the morphological diversity within this genus. Plants are of the same age and relative size differences are conserved in the pictures.</p>
</div>
          
          
              </figcaption>
          
          
          
          
          </figure>
    
    
    </div>
<p class="paragraph">The concatenated ExaML species tree and the ASTRAL species tree shared overall similar topologies, but very short internal branch lengths on both trees indicated high levels of gene tree discordance. We further dissected this discordance by assessing support of the main topology of the ASTRAL species tree (<a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig5s1">Figure 5figure supplement 1</a>) using quartet scores, which compare the main tree topology relative to its first and second alternative topology. For most nodes in the ingroup, each topology had quartet scores near the minimum value of 1/3 (<a href="/articles/51712/figures#supp4">Supplementary file 4</a>), indicating that the possible gene trees were present in almost equal frequency for each topology. For the ExaML tree, we assessed discordance at each node using concordance factors, which are the proportion of gene trees that agree with the main topology (<a href="#fig5">Figure 5</a>). Again, most nodes in the ingroup had very low support for the main topology (&lt;5% of gene tree agreement; <a href="#fig5">Figure 5</a>, <a href="/articles/51712/figures#supp5">Supplementary file 5</a>). This suggests that many internal branches had lengths near 0, indicating polytomies that could not be resolved even with the extensive sampling of gene sequences from transcriptome data. These high levels of discordance were likely caused by frequent polyploidization (<a href="#fig5">Figure 5</a>), incomplete lineage sorting, and high degrees of hybridization. A high prevalence of hybridization was further indicated by a high frequency of gamma scores (hybridization proportion) between 0.3 and 0.7 across all ingroup taxa (<a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig5s2">Figure 5figure supplement 2</a>) recovered in the HyDe analysis (<a href="#bib17">Blischak et al., 2018</a>).</p>
<p class="paragraph">Despite extensive levels of discordance and low agreement of individual gene trees with the species trees, the main topologies of the ExaML and ASTRAL species trees revealed geographic clades that matched the generally limited native species ranges. The three Mediterranean annual species <i>E. incanum</i> (INC), <i>E. repandum</i> (REP), and <i>E. wilczekianum</i> (WIC) formed a well-supported monophyletic sister clade to all other sequenced species (<a href="#fig5">Figure 5</a>, <a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig5s1">Figure 5figure supplement 1</a>). The only other annual in the set of sampled species, <i>E. cheiranthoides</i> (ECE), was part of a weakly-supported clade (high posterior probability but low concordance), comprised of several perennial species from Greece and central Europe, including the widespread ornamental <i>E. cheiri</i> (CHR). Species from the Iberian peninsula/Morocco, North America, and Iran formed additional, weakly-supported clades conserved between species trees (<a href="#fig5">Figure 5</a>), while another clade of Turkish and Greek <i>Erysimum</i> species was only monophyletic in the ASTRAL species tree (<a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig5s1">Figure 5figure supplement 1</a>). The clear geographic structure in the main topologies of the species trees was confirmed by a strong correlation between the cophenetic and geographic distance matrices for the subset of 43 species with geographic information (Mantel test, p&lt;0.001).</p>




  </div>

</section>
<section
    class="article-section "
   id="s2-4"
  
  
>

  <header class="article-section__header">
    <h3 class="article-section__header_text">Glucosinolate diversity and myrosinase activity</h3>
  </header>

  <div class="article-section__body">
      <p class="paragraph">Across the 48 <i>Erysimum</i> species, we identified 25 candidate glucosinolate compounds with distinct molecular masses and HPLC retention times (<a href="/articles/51712/figures#supp6">Supplementary file 6</a>). Of these, 24 compounds could be assigned to known glucosinolate structures with high certainty. The last remaining compound appeared to be an unknown isomer of glucocheirolin. Individual <i>Erysimum</i> species produced between 5 and 18 glucosinolates (<a href="#fig6">Figure 6A</a>), and total glucosinolate concentrations were highly variable among species (<a href="#fig6">Figure 6B</a>). The ploidy level of species explained a significant fraction of total variation in the number of glucosinolates produced (F<sub>4,38</sub> = 4.63, p=0.004), with hexaploid species producing the highest number of compounds (<a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig6s1">Figure 6figure supplement 1</a>). However, neither the number of distinct glucosinolate compounds nor their total concentrations exhibited a phylogenetic signal, and related species were less similar than expected under a model of Brownian motion (<a href="#table2">Table 2</a>).</p>

<p class="paragraph">Clustering species by dissimilarities in glucosinolate profiles mostly resulted in chemotype groups corresponding to known underlying biosynthetic genes, although support for individual species clusters in the chemogram was variable (<a href="#fig7">Figure 7</a>). The majority of all species produced glucoiberin as the primary glucosinolate. Of these, approximately half also produced sinigrin as a second dominant glucosinolate compound. Further chemotypic subdivision, related to the production of glucocheirolin and 2-hydroxypropyl glucosinolate, appeared to be present but only had relatively weak statistical support. However, eight species clearly differed from these general patterns. The species <i>E. allionii</i> (ALI), <i>E. rhaeticum</i> (RHA), and <i>E. scoparium</i> (SCO) mostly lacked glucosinolates with 3-carbon side-chains, but instead accumulated glucosinolates with 4-, 5- and 6-carbon side-chains. The two closely related species <i>E. odoratum</i> (ODO) and <i>E. witmannii</i> (WIT) predominantly accumulated indole glucosinolates, while <i>E. collinum</i> (COL), <i>E. pulchellum</i> (PUL), and accession ER2 predominantly produced glucoerypestrin (3-methoxycarbonylpropyl glucosinolate), a glucosinolate that is exclusively found within <i>Erysimum</i> (<a href="#bib47">Fahey et al., 2001</a>).</p>
    <div
        id="fig7"
        class="asset-viewer-inline asset-viewer-inline-- "

        data-behaviour="AssetNavigation AssetViewer ToggleableCaption"
        data-selector=".caption-text__body"

              <figcaption class="captioned-asset__caption">
          
                  <h6 class="caption-text__heading">Glucosinolate compound diversity and abundance across 48 <i>Erysimum</i>species.</h6>
                
                
                <div class="caption-text__body"><p class="paragraph">(<b>A</b>) Chemogram clustering species by dissimilarities in glucosinolate profiles.Values at nodes are confidence estimates (approximately unbiased probability value, function <i>pvclust</i> in R) based on 10,000 iterations of multiscale bootstrap resampling. (<b>B</b>) Heatmap of glucosinolate profiles expressed by the 48 <i>Erysimum</i> species. Color intensity corresponds to log-transformed integrated ion counts recorded at the exact parental mass ([M-H]<sup>-</sup>) for each compound, averaged across samples from multiple independent experiments. Compounds are grouped by major biosynthetic classes and labelled using systematic short names. See <a href="/articles/51712/figures#supp6">Supplementary file 6</a> for full glucosinolate names and additional compound information. (<b>C</b>) Classification of species chemotype based on predominant glucosinolate compounds. 3C/4C/5C=length of carbon side chain, MSI=methylsulfinyl glucosinolate, MSO=methylsulfonyl glucosinolate, OH=side chain with hydroxy group, ALK=side chain with alkenyl group, CARB=carboxylic glucosinolate, IND=indole glucosinolate.</p>
</div>
          
          
              </figcaption>
          
          
          
          
          </figure>
    
    
    </div>
<p class="paragraph">Similar to the lack of phylogenetic signal for compound numbers and concentrations, dissimilarity in glucosinolate profiles was unrelated to phylogenetic relatedness (Mantel test, p=0.331), and neither of the first two principal coordinates of the glucosinolate dissimilarity matrix showed a significant phylogenetic signal (<a href="#table2">Table 2</a>). The lack of phylogenetic signal was visualized by optimizing vertical matching of tips between the ExaML species tree and the glucosinolate chemogram (<a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig5s3">Figure 5figure supplement 3A</a>). For five species pairs, the closest phylogenetic neighbor was also the most chemically similar species, but in general, close relatives more often belonged to chemically distant species clusters. Finally, reconstruction of the ancestral states for total glucosinolate content and the first principal coordinate of the glucosinolate dissimilarity matrix suggests that both traits likely originated at intermediate levels and repeatedly evolved towards opposite extremes in closely related species (<a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig5s4">Figure 5figure supplement 4</a>).</p>
<p class="paragraph">As glucosinolates require activation by myrosinase enzymes upon tissue damage by herbivores, myrosinase activity in leaf tissue determines the rate at which toxins are released. We quantified myrosinase activity of <i>Erysimum</i> leaf extracts and found it to be highly variable among species (<a href="#fig6">Figure 6C</a>). After grouping species into nine chemotypes defined by chemical dissimilarity and the production of characteristic glucosinolate compounds (<a href="#fig7">Figure 7C</a>), we found that myrosinase activity significantly differed among these chemotypes (<a href="#fig8">Figure 8</a>, F<sub>8,33</sub> = 8.31, p&lt;0.001). Chemotypes that predominantly accumulated methylsulfonyl glucosinolates, hydroxy glucosinolates, or indole glucosinolates had low to negligible activity against the assayed glucosinolate sinigrin. It is important to note that sinigrin is an alkenyl glucosinolate and <i>Erysimum</i> myrosinases targeting other, structurally dissimilar glucosinolates may not effectively cleave sinigrin. After chemotype differences were accounted for, myrosinase activity was marginally related to total glucosinolate concentrations (F<sub>1,33</sub> = 3.60, p=0.067). Similar to other glucosinolate traits, uncorrected myrosinase activity exhibited no phylogenetic signal (<a href="#table2">Table 2</a>).</p>

        data-asset-viewer-height="1366"

          <div class="asset-viewer-inline__header_text">

<section
    class="article-section "
   id="s2-5"
  
  
>

  <header class="article-section__header">
    <h3 class="article-section__header_text">Cardenolide diversity</h3>
  </header>

  <div class="article-section__body">
      <p class="paragraph">With the exception of <i>E. collinum</i> (COL), which only contained trace amounts of cardenolides in leaves, all <i>Erysimum</i> species contained diverse mixtures of cardenolide compounds and accumulated considerable amounts of cardenolides (<a href="#fig6">Figure 6DE</a>). The ploidy level of species again explained a significant fraction of the total variation in the number of cardenolides (F<sub>4,38</sub> = 3.47, p=0.016), with hexaploid species producing the highest average number of compounds (<a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig6s1">Figure 6figure supplement 1</a>). To obtain an estimate of biological activity and evaluate quantification from total MS ion counts, we used an established assay that quantifies cardenolide concentrations from specific inhibition of animal Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase by crude <i>Erysimum</i> leaf extracts. We found generally strong enzymatic inhibition, with leaves of <i>Erysimum</i> species on average containing an equivalent of 5.720.12 g mg<sup>1</sup> (1 SE) of the reference cardenolide ouabain. Despite only producing trace amounts of cardenolides, <i>E. collinum</i> (COL) extracts caused significantly stronger inhibition than the Brassicaceae control plant, <i>S. arvensis</i> (<a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig6s2">Figure 6figure supplement 2</a>). Overall, quantification of cardenolide concentrations by Na<sup>+</sup>/K<sup>+</sup>-ATPase inhibition was highly correlated with the total MS ion count (<a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig6s2">Figure 6figure supplement 2</a>, r=0.95, p&lt;0.001). Thus, the use of ion count data for cross-species comparisons was appropriate for this purpose. Both the total numbers of compounds and the total abundances exhibited a strong phylogenetic signal (<a href="#table2">Table 2</a>), indicating that closely related species shared similar cardenolide traits.</p>
<p class="paragraph">Cardenolide diversity was considerably higher than that of glucosinolates, with a total of 97 distinguishable candidate cardenolide compounds identified across the 48 <i>Erysimum</i> species (two compounds were later excluded,leaving95compounds; <a href="/articles/51712/figures#supp7">Supplementary file 7</a>). Of these, 46 compounds had distinct molecular masses and mass fragments, while the remaining compounds likely were isomers, sharing a molecular mass with other compounds but having distinct HPLC retention times. The 95 putative cardenolides comprised nine distinct genins (<a href="#fig9">Figure 9</a>, <a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig9s1">Figure 9figure supplement 1</a>), the majority of which were glycosylated with digitoxose, deoxy hexoses, xylose, or glucose moieties. Only digitoxigenin and cannogenol accumulated as free genins, while all other compounds occurred as either mono- or di-glycosides. A likely major source of isomeric cardenolide compounds was thus the incorporation of different deoxy hexoses of equivalent mass, such as rhamnose, fucose, or gulomethylose. A subset of compounds had molecular masses that were heavier by 42.011 m/z than known mono- or di-glycoside cardenolides. Such a gain in mass corresponds to the gain of an acetyl-group, and mass fragmentation patterns indicated that these compounds were acetylated on the first sugar moiety (<a href="/articles/51712/figures#supp7">Supplementary file 7</a>). Out of the nine detected genins, six had previously been described from <i>Erysimum</i> species (<a href="#bib112">Makarevich et al., 1994</a>). In addition, we identified three previously undescribed mass features with fragmentation patterns characteristic of cardenolide genins (<a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig9s1">Figure 9figure supplement 1</a>). Of these three, one matched an acetylated cannogenol, one matched formylated cannogenol, and one matched formylated nigrescigenin, assuming acetylation/formylation of a free OH-group on the precursor molecule. Formyl adducts can sometimes be formed during LC-MS due to the addition of formic acid to solvents, although this is less common with positive ionization. To exclude the possibility that these were technical artefacts, we analyzed a subset of extracts by LC-MS without the addition of formic acid and found both formyl-genins at comparable concentrations (<a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig9s2">Figure 9figure supplement 2</a>). We therefore assume that all three novel structures are natural variants of cardenolides produced by <i>Erysimum</i> plants, even though we currently lack final structural elucidation.</p>
    <div
        id="fig9"
        class="asset-viewer-inline asset-viewer-inline-- "
        data-variant=""
        data-behaviour="AssetNavigation AssetViewer ToggleableCaption"

        data-asset-viewer-height="1500"

                  <source srcset="https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:51712%2Felife-51712-fig9-v2.tif/full/1234,/0/default.webp 2x, https://iiif.elifesciences.org/lax:51712%2Felife-51712-fig9-v2.tif/full/617,/0/default.webp 1x"

                       alt=""
                       class="captioned-asset__image"
                  >
              </picture>
              </a>
          
          
          
          
          
              <figcaption class="captioned-asset__caption">
          
                  <h6 class="caption-text__heading">Predicted pathways of cardenolide genin modification in <i>Erysimum</i>.</h6>
                
                
                <div class="caption-text__body"><p class="paragraph">Pathways are linearized for simplicity but more likely form a complex network. Genin diversity likely originates from digitoxigenin, which is transformed into more stucturally complex cardenolides by hydroxylases, dehydrogenases, and formyl-, methyl-, or acetyltransferases. Acetyl-cannogenol could be derived directly from cannogenol or from formyl-cannogenol, with the frequent co-occurrence of acetyl-cannogenol and formyl-cannogenol in leaf extracts suggesting the latter. According to their exact mass, frequently detected dihydroxy-digitoxigenin compounds (C<sub>23</sub>H<sub>34</sub>O<sub>6</sub>) could be either bipindogenin or strophanthidol (grayed out). While bipindogenin cardenolides have commonly been reported for <i>Erysimum</i> species in the literature, their structure would require additional intermediates that have not been detected (n.d.). Thus, strophanthidol appears to be the more likely isomer to occur in <i>Erysimum</i>. All cardenolide genins are further modified by glycosylation at a conserved position in the molecule (R). Note that all structures are putative, and particularly formyl- and acetyl-modifications could be attached to any free OH-group.</p>
</div>
          
          
              </figcaption>
          
          
          
          
          </figure>
    
    
    </div>
<p class="paragraph">Clustering of species by dissimilarities in cardenolide profiles revealed fewer obvious species clusters in the chemogram than for glucosinolates, and particularly higher-level species clusters had only weak statistical support (<a href="#fig10">Figure 10</a>). A clear exception to this was a species cluster that included <i>E. cheiranthoides</i> (ECE) and <i>E. sylvestre</i> (SYL), which were characterized by a chemotype lacking several otherwise common cannogenol- and strophanthidin-glycosides, while accumulating unique digitoxigenin-glycosides. A second major cluster was visually apparent, yet not statistically significant, and separated groups of species that did or did not produce glycosides of the newly discovered putative formyl-nigrescigenin (<a href="#fig10">Figure 10</a>). Similarity in cardenolide profiles among species was strongly correlated with phylogenetic relatedness (Mantel test, p&lt;0.001), and the first two principal coordinates of the Bray-Curtis dissimilarity matrix exhibited strong phylogenetic signals (<a href="#table2">Table 2</a>). Closely related species were therefore not only more similar in their total cardenolide concentrations, but also had more similar cardenolide profiles than expected by chance. These results were again visualized by optimizing vertical matching of tips between the ExaML species tree and the cardenolide chemogram (<a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig5s3">Figure 5figure supplement 3B</a>). For twelve species pairs (half of all species in our phylogeny), the closest phylogenetic neighbor was also the most chemically similar species, and phylogenetically related species more commonly belonged to chemically similar species clusters, indicated by a significantly lower total length of tip links compared to what was observed for glucosinolates (<a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig5s3">Figure 5figure supplement 3B</a>). Reconstruction of the ancestral states for total cardenolide content suggests that trait values likely originated at low total concentrations, and increased to intermediate levels in the North American and Spanish/Moroccan clades, and independently to very high levels in the species pair of <i>E. horizontale</i> and <i>E. crepidifolium</i> (<a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig5s4">Figure 5figure supplement 4</a>). For the first principal coordinate of cardenolide dissimilarity, trait values originated at intermediate values, but sub-clades more commonly evolved towards shared chemical profiles than was the case for glucosinolates (<a href="/articles/51712/figures#fig5s4">Figure 5figure supplement 4</a>).</p>

                  <h6 class="caption-text__heading">Cardenolide compound diversity and abundance accross 48 <i>Erysimum</i>species.</h6>
                
                
                <div class="caption-text__body"><p class="paragraph">(<b>A</b>) Chemogram clustering species by dissimilarities in cardenolide profiles.Values at nodes are confidence estimates (approximately unbiased probability value, function <i>pvclust</i> in R) based on 10,000 iterations of multiscale bootstrap resampling. (<b>B</b>) Heatmap of cardenolide profiles expressed by the 48 <i>Erysimum</i> species. Color intensity corresponds to log-transformed integrated ion counts recorded at the exact parental mass ([M+H]<sup>+</sup> or [M+Na]<sup>+</sup>, whichever was more abundant) for each compound, averaged across samples from multiple independent experiments. The species <i>E. collinum</i> (COL) only expressed trace amounts of cardenolides, which are not visible on the color scale. Compounds are grouped by shared genin structures. Cgi.=Cannogenin, For-can.=Formyl cannogenol, Ac-can.=Acetyl cannogenol. See <a href="/articles/51712/figures#supp7">Supplementary file 7</a> for additional compound information.</p>

</section>
<section
    class="article-section "
   id="s2-6"
  
  
>

  <header class="article-section__header">
    <h3 class="article-section__header_text">Macroevolutionary patterns in defense and inducibility</h3>
  </header>

  <div class="article-section__body">
      <p class="paragraph">Similarity in glucosinolate and cardenolide chemical profiles of the 48 species was not correlated (Mantel test, p=0.171), and neither the number of compounds (PGLS: F<sub>1,46</sub> = 0.09, p=0.771) nor their total concentrations (PGLS: F<sub>1,46</sub> = 0.51, p=0.478) were correlated between compound classes. Tip-specific estimates of speciation rates were not correlated with the number of glucosinolate compounds produced by a species, regardless of speciation rate metric or statistical method used (<a href="#table3">Table 3</a>). In contrast, we found a significantly positive correlation between the node density (ND) measure and the number of cardenolide compounds, while for the alternate equal split (ES) measure the correlation was marginally significant for the simulation-based method only (<a href="#table3">Table 3</a>). Given the correlation coefficients of the simulation-based method, variation in the number of cardenolide compounds thus explained 1728% of the total variation in speciation rate. Variation in total glucosinolate or cardenolide concentrations was not correlated with speciation rates (<a href="#table3">Table 3</a>).</p>
    <div
        id="table3"
        class="asset-viewer-inline asset-viewer-inline--table "
        data-variant="table"
        data-behaviour="AssetNavigation AssetViewer ToggleableCaption"
        data-selector=".caption-text__body"
        data-asset-viewer-group="table3"
    >
    
      <div class="asset-viewer-inline__header_panel">
          <div class="asset-viewer-inline__header_text">
            <span class="asset-viewer-inline__header_text__prominent">Table 3</span>
          </div>
    
    
            <div class="asset-viewer-inline__figure_access">
            </div>
    
      </div>
    
          <figure class="captioned-asset">
          
          
          
          
          
          
              <figcaption class="captioned-asset__caption">
          
                  <h6 class="caption-text__heading">Correlations between plant traits and tip-specific speciation rates estimated from the main ExaML species tree.</h6>
                
                
                <div class="caption-text__body"><p class="paragraph">Each trait is correlated against two estimates of speciation rates using phylogenetic least squares (PGLS) and a simulation-based method (SIM). Node density (ND) and equal split (ES) estimates of speciation rates are strongly correlated (r=0.767, p&lt;0.001) but differ in relative weighting of recent and more distant evolutionary history. For 1000 sets of randomly generated traits, only three resulted in more than one significant correlation, suggesting that multiple significant tests per trait (bold) are unlikely to arise by chance.</p>
</div>
          
          
              </figcaption>
          
          
          
              <div class="table">
              
              
                  <div class="table__table">
              
                    <table><thead><tr><th valign="top"/><th valign="top">Nd-pgls</th><th valign="top">Nd-sim</th><th valign="top">Es-pgls</th><th valign="top">Es-sim</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td valign="top">Total glucosinolate concentrations</td><td valign="top">F<sub>1,46</sub> = 0.79, <br/>p=0.379</td><td valign="top">r<sub>Pearson</sub>=0.29, <br/>p=0.300</td><td valign="top">F<sub>1,46</sub> = 0.00, <br/>p=0.990</td><td valign="top">r<sub>Pearson</sub>=0.09, <br/>p=0.737</td></tr><tr><td valign="top">Number of glucosinolate compounds</td><td valign="top">F<sub>1,46</sub> = 1.16, <br/>p=0.286</td><td valign="top">r<sub>Pearson</sub>=0.23, <br/>p=0.412</td><td valign="top">F<sub>1,46</sub> = 0.87, <br/>p=0.356</td><td valign="top">r<sub>Pearson</sub>=0.14, <br/>p=0.603</td></tr><tr><td valign="top">Total cardenolide concentrations</td><td valign="top">F<sub>1,46</sub> = 0.29, <br/>p=0.593</td><td valign="top">r<sub>Pearson</sub>=0.25, <br/>p=0.373</td><td valign="top">F<sub>1,46</sub> = 0.01, <br/>p=0.908</td><td valign="top">r<sub>Pearson</sub>=0.23, <br/>p=0.398</td></tr><tr><td valign="top">Number of cardenolide compounds</td><td valign="top"><b>F<sub>1,46</sub>=5.87,</b> <br/><b>p=0.019</b></td><td valign="top"><b>r<sub>Pearson</sub>=0.53,</b> <br/><b>p=0.030</b></td><td valign="top">F<sub>1,46</sub> = 1.93, <br/>p=0.17</td><td valign="top">r<sub>Pearson</sub>=0.42, <br/>p=0.093</td></tr></tbody></table>
              
                  </div>
              
              
              
              </div>
          
          </figure>
    
    
    </div>
<p class="paragraph">Foliar application of JA was expected to stimulate accumulation of defensive compounds in plant leaves and among the 30 tested species, glucosinolate levels responded positively to JA, with the majority of species increasing their foliar glucosinolate concentration (<a href="#fig11">Figure 11</a>). However, the glucosinolate inducibility of a species was independent of constitutive glucosinolate levels (PGLS: F<sub>1,28</sub> = 0.17, p=0.680). By contrast, the majority of species exhibited lower cardenolide levels in response to JA, resulting in lack of inducibility across species (<a href="#fig11">Figure 11</a>). The species <i>E. crepidifolium</i> (CRE) heavily influenced inducibility patterns, as it not only had three times higher constitutive concentrations of cardenolides than any other <i>Erysimum</i> species, but also markedly increased both glucosinolate and cardenolide concentrations in response to JA treatment (<a href="#fig11">Figure 11</a>). When this outlier species was removed, inducibility (or suppression) of foliar cardenolides was not correlated with constitutive cardenolide levels (PGLS: F<sub>1,27</sub> = 0.20, p=0.657), and inducibilities of glucosinolates and cardenolides were likewise not correlated with each other (PGLS: F<sub>1,27</sub> = 0.36, p=0.551).</p>

        class="asset-viewer-inline asset-viewer-inline-- "

        data-asset-viewer-width="1500"
        data-asset-viewer-height="1479"
    >
    
      <div class="asset-viewer-inline__header_panel">
          <div class="asset-viewer-inline__header_text">
            <span class="asset-viewer-inline__header_text__prominent">Figure 11</span>
          </div>
